material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

4.7

Condition 5 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be produced or access to it
should be given having regard to—

4.7.a

the benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is obtained,
and

4.7.b

the circumstances under which the person concerned is likely to have any of
the material in their possession, custody or control.

4.8

An order under  this paragraph  is an order that the person specified in the
application must do any of the following—

4.8.a

notify a named constable as soon as reasonably practicable after any material
to which the application relates comes into the person’s possession, custody
or control;

4.8.b

produce to a constable within a specified period for seizure and retention any
material to which the application relates which comes into the person’s
possession, custody or control;

4.8.c

give a constable access to any material of the kind mentioned in paragraph
(b)  within a specified period;

4.8.d

state to the best of the person’s knowledge and belief the location of the
material to which the application relates if it is not in, and will not come
into, the person’s possession, custody or control within the period of 28 days
beginning with the date of the order.

4.9

The specified period is to be the period of 7 days beginning with the date of
the notification required by sub-paragraph  (8) (a) , unless it appears to the
judge that a different period would be appropriate in the particular
circumstances of the application.

###### Production orders: supplementary provision

5

An application for an order under paragraph  3  or  4  may be made without
notice to a judge in chambers.

6

6.1

An order under  paragraph 3  or  4 —

6.1.a

does not confer any right to production of, or access to, items subject to
legal privilege, and

6.1.b

has effect despite any restriction on the disclosure of information imposed by
an enactment or otherwise.

6.2

Where the material consists of information stored in electronic form—

6.2.a

an order under  paragraph 3 (7) (a)  or  4 (8) (b)  has effect as an order to
produce the material in a form in which it can be taken away and in which it
is visible and legible, or from which it can readily be produced in a visible
and legible form, and

6.2.b

an order under  paragraph 3 (7) (b)  or  4 (8) (c)  has effect as an order to
give a constable access to the material in a form in which it is visible and
legible.

7

7.1

An order under  paragraph 3  or  4  may be made in relation to material in the
possession, custody or control of a government department or a Northern
Ireland department.

7.2

Where an order is made by virtue of  sub-paragraph (1) —

7.2.a

it is to be served as if the proceedings were civil proceedings against the
department, and

7.2.b

it may require any officer of the department, whether named in the order or
not, who may for the time being have in their possession, custody or control
the material concerned, to comply with the order.

7.3

In  this paragraph  “ government department ” means an authorised government
department for the purposes of the Crown Proceedings Act 1947.

8

An order under paragraph  3  or  4  has effect as if it were an order of the
court.

###### Confidential material: search, seizure and retention

9

9.1

A constable may apply to a judge for the issue of a warrant under  this
paragraph .

9.2

The judge may grant the application if satisfied that an order made under
paragraph 3  or  4  in relation to material on the relevant premises has not
been complied with.

9.3

The judge may also grant the application if satisfied that—

9.3.a

conditions 1 to 5 are met, and

9.3.b

in the case of an application for an all premises warrant, condition 6 is met.

9.4

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

9.5

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that there is
on the relevant premises material which—

9.5.a

is likely to be evidence that a relevant act has been, or is about to be,
committed,

9.5.b

consists of or includes confidential material, and

9.5.c

does not include items subject to legal privilege.

9.6

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that the
material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

9.7

Condition 4 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be obtained having regard to—

9.7.a

the benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is obtained,
and

9.7.b

the circumstances under which the person concerned has any of the material in
their possession, custody or control.

9.8

Condition 5 is that any of the following apply—

9.8.a

it is not practicable to communicate with any person entitled to produce the
material;

9.8.b

it is not practicable to communicate with any person entitled to grant access
to the material;

9.8.c

the investigation may be seriously prejudiced unless a constable can secure
immediate access to the material.

9.9

Condition 6 is that it is not reasonably practicable to specify in the
application all the premises which the person specified in the application
occupies or controls and which might need to be searched.

9.10

A warrant under  this paragraph  is a warrant authorising any constable—

9.10.a

to enter the relevant premises,

9.10.b

to search the relevant premises and any person found there, and

9.10.c

to seize and retain any material found on a search under  paragraph (b)  which
is likely to be evidence that a relevant act has been, or is about to be,
committed.

9.11

A warrant under  this paragraph  does not authorise—

9.11.a

the seizure and retention of items subject to legal privilege, or

9.11.b

a constable to require a person to remove any clothing in public except for
headgear, footwear, an outer coat or jacket and gloves.

9.12

In  this paragraph  the “relevant premises” are—

9.12.a

one or more sets of premises specified in the application (in which case the
application is for a “specific premises warrant”), or

9.12.b

any premises occupied or controlled by a person specified in the application,
including such sets of premises as are so specified (in which case the
application is for an “all premises warrant”).

###### Explanations

10

10.1

A constable may apply to a judge for an order under  this paragraph  requiring
any person specified in the order to provide an explanation of material—

10.1.a

seized under a warrant under  paragraph 2  or  9 , or

10.1.b

produced or made available to a constable under  paragraph 3  or  4 .

10.2

An application for an order under this paragraph may be made without notice to
a judge in chambers.

10.3

An order under  this paragraph  may not require any person to disclose any
information which they would be entitled to refuse to disclose on grounds of
legal professional privilege in proceedings in the High Court.

10.4

But a lawyer may be required to provide the name and address of their client.

10.5

A statement by a person in response to a requirement imposed by an order under
this paragraph —

10.5.a

may be made orally or in writing, and

10.5.b

may be used in evidence against the person only on a prosecution for an
offence under  paragraph 11 .

10.6

An order under this paragraph has effect as if it were an order of the court.

11

11.1

A person commits an offence if, in purported compliance with an order under
paragraph 10 , they—

11.1.a

make a statement which they know to be false or misleading in a material
particular, or

11.1.b

recklessly make a statement which is false or misleading in a material
particular.

11.2

A person who commits an offence under  sub-paragraph (1)  is liable—

11.2.a

on conviction on indictment, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding two
years or a fine (or both),

11.2.b

on summary conviction in England and Wales, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding the general limit in a magistrates’ court or a fine (or both);

11.2.c

on summary conviction in Northern Ireland, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding 6 months or a fine not exceeding the statutory maximum (or both).

###### Urgent cases

12

12.1

A police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may by a written order
signed by them give to any constable the authority which may be given by—

12.1.a

a warrant under  paragraph 2 , or

12.1.b

a warrant under paragraph  9 ,

but subject to  sub-paragraph (2) .

12.2

An order under  this paragraph  giving the authority which may be given by a
warrant under  paragraph 9  does not authorise a constable to retain
confidential journalistic material.

12.3

An officer may not make an order under  this paragraph  unless the officer —

12.3.a

is satisfied as mentioned in  paragraph 2 (2)  or paragraph  9 (2)  or  (3)
(as the case may be), and

12.3.b

has reasonable grounds for believing that the case is one of great emergency
and that immediate action is necessary.

12.4

Where an order is made under  this paragraph  particulars of the case must be
notified as soon as is reasonably practicable to the Secretary of State.

12.5

A person who wilfully obstructs a search under  this paragraph  commits an
offence.

12.6

A person who commits an offence under sub-paragraph  (5)  is liable on summary
conviction to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 3 months or a fine not
exceeding level 4 on the standard scale (or both).

13

13.1

This paragraph  applies where confidential journalistic material is seized by
virtue of an order under  paragraph 12  giving the authority which may be
given by a warrant under  paragraph 9 .

13.2

A constable may apply to a judge for the issue of a warrant under  this
paragraph .

13.3

An application under  sub-paragraph (2)  must be made as soon as reasonably
practicable after the material is seized.

13.4

The judge may grant an application under  sub-paragraph (2)  if satisfied that
conditions 1 to 3 are met.

13.5

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

13.6

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that the
material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

13.7

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be retained having regard to the
benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is retained.

13.8

A warrant under  this paragraph  is a warrant authorising the retention of
confidential journalistic material.

13.9

A warrant under  this paragraph  may impose conditions on the retention and
use of the material.

13.10

If the judge does not grant an application for the issue of a warrant under
this paragraph  in relation to any of the material to which the application
relates, the judge may direct that the material is—

13.10.a

returned to the person from whom it was seized, or

13.10.b

destroyed.

14

14.1

If a police officer of at least the rank of superintendent has reasonable
grounds for believing that the case is one of great emergency the officer may
by a written notice signed by them require any person specified in the notice
to provide an explanation of any material seized in pursuance of an order
under paragraph  12 .

14.2

Paragraph  10 (3)  to  (5)  and paragraph  11  apply to a notice under  this
paragraph  as they apply to an order under paragraph  10 .

14.3

A person who fails to comply with a notice under  this paragraph  commits an
offence.

14.4

It is a defence for a person charged with an offence under  sub-paragraph (3)
to show that they had a reasonable excuse for their failure.

14.5

A person is taken to have shown that they had a reasonable excuse for their
failure if—

14.5.a

sufficient evidence of that fact is adduced to raise an issue with respect to
it, and

14.5.b

the contrary is not proved beyond reasonable doubt.

14.6

A person guilty of an offence under  sub-paragraph (3)  is liable—

14.6.a

on summary conviction in England and Wales, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding the maximum term for summary offences or a fine (or both);

14.6.b

on summary conviction in Northern Ireland, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding 6 months or a fine not exceeding level 5 on the standard scale (or
both).

###### Application of PACE

15

15.1

For the purposes of the provisions mentioned in  sub-paragraph (2) —

15.1.a

an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is about to be,
committed is to be treated as an investigation into, or in connection with, an
offence, and

15.1.b

material produced in pursuance of an order under  paragraph 3  or  4  is to be
treated as if it were material seized by a constable.

15.2

The provisions are—

15.2.a

sections 21 and 22 of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984;

15.2.b

Articles 23 and 24 of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland)
Order 1989 ( S.I. 1989/1341 (N.I. 12) ),

(seized material: access, copying and retention).

###### Procedure rules

16

16.1

Criminal Procedure Rules may make provision about proceedings in England and
Wales relating to a warrant or order under  this Part  of  this Schedule
(other than an order under paragraph  12 ).

16.2

Crown Court rules and magistrates’ courts rules may make provision about
proceedings in Northern Ireland relating to a warrant or order under  this
Part  of  this Schedule  (other than an order under paragraph  12 ).

16.3

The provision which may be made by virtue of  this paragraph  includes in
particular provision about the variation or discharge of an order.

###### Interpretation

17

17.1

This paragraph applies for the interpretation of this Part of this Schedule.

17.2

“ Confidential material ” means—

17.2.a

confidential journalistic material, and

17.2.b

protected material.

17.3

“ Confidential journalistic material ” has the same meaning as in the
Investigatory Powers Act 2016 (see section 264(6) and (7) of that Act).

17.4

“Protected material”—

17.4.a

in relation to England and Wales, means—

17.4.a.i

items subject to legal privilege,

17.4.a.ii

material falling within section 11(1)(a) or (b) of the Police and Criminal
Evidence Act 1984 (certain personal records, human tissue or tissue fluid held
in confidence), or

17.4.a.iii

material to which section 14(2) of that Act applies (other material acquired
in the course of a trade etc that is held in confidence);

17.4.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, means—

17.4.b.i

items subject to legal privilege;

17.4.b.ii

material falling with Article 13(1)(a) or (b) of the Police and Criminal
Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989 ( S.I. 1989/1341 (N.I. 12) ) (certain
personal records, human tissue or tissue fluid held in confidence), or

17.4.b.iii

material to which Article 16(2) of that Order applies (other material acquired
in the course of a trade etc that is held in confidence).

17.5

“Items subject to legal privilege”—

17.5.a

in relation to England and Wales, has the meaning given by section 10 of the
Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984;

17.5.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, has the meaning given by Article 12 of the
Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989 ( S.I. 1989/1341
(N.I. 12) ).

17.6

“Judge”—

17.6.a

in relation to England and Wales, means a judge entitled to exercise the
jurisdiction of the Crown Court;

17.6.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, means a judge of the Crown Court.

##### Scotland

###### Introductory

18

18.1

This Part  of  this Schedule  applies in Scotland.

18.2

In  this Part  of  this Schedule  “ relevant act ” means—

18.2.a

an offence under  this Part  of this Act, other than an offence under—

18.2.a.i

section  5  (unauthorised entry to a prohibited place);

18.2.a.ii

section  6  (prohibited place: failure to comply with order of constable);

18.2.a.iii

section  11  (cordoned area: failure to comply with order of constable);

18.2.a.iv

this Schedule ;

18.2.a.v

Schedule  3  (disclosure orders);

18.2.a.vi

Schedule  4  (customer information orders);

18.2.b

an act or threat within section  33 (3) (b)  or  (c) .

###### Production orders

19

19.1

The procurator fiscal may apply to a sheriff for an order under  this
paragraph .

19.2

The sheriff may grant the application if satisfied that conditions 1 to 4 are
met.

19.3

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

19.4

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a person
specified in the application has in their possession, custody or control
material which is likely to be evidence that a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

19.5

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that the
material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

19.6

Condition 4 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be produced or access to it
should be given having regard to—

19.6.a

the benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is obtained,
and

19.6.b

the circumstances under which the person concerned has any of the material in
their possession, custody or control.

19.7

An order under  this paragraph  is an order that the person specified in the
application must do any of the following—

19.7.a

produce to a constable within a specified period for seizure and retention any
material which the person has in their possession, custody or control and to
which the application relates;

19.7.b

give a constable access to any material of the kind mentioned in  paragraph
(a)  within a specified period;

19.7.c

state to the best of the person’s knowledge and belief the location of the
material to which the application relates if it is not in, and will not come
into, the person’s possession, custody or control within the period specified
under  paragraph (a)  or  (b) .

19.8

The specified period is to be the period of 7 days beginning with the date of
the order, unless it appears to the sheriff that a different period would be
appropriate in the particular circumstances of the application.

19.9

Where the sheriff makes an order under  sub-paragraph (7) (b)  in relation to
material on any premises, they may, on the application of the procurator
fiscal, order any person who appears to them to be entitled to grant entry to
the premises to allow any constable to enter the premises to obtain access to
the material.

20

20.1

The procurator fiscal may apply to a sheriff for an order under  this
paragraph .

20.2

The sheriff may grant the application if satisfied that conditions 1 to 5 are
met.

20.3

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

20.4

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that, within
the period of 28 days beginning with the date of the order, there is likely to
come into existence material which is evidence that a relevant act has been,
or is about to be, committed.

20.5

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a person
specified in the application is likely within that period to have in their
possession, custody or control any of the material to which the application
relates.

20.6

Condition 4 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that the
material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

20.7

Condition 5 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be produced or access to it
should be given having regard to—

20.7.a

the benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is obtained,
and

20.7.b

the circumstances under which the person concerned is likely to have any of
the material in their possession, custody or control.

20.8

An order under  this paragraph  is an order that the person specified in the
application must do any of the following—

20.8.a

notify a named constable as soon as reasonably practicable after any material
to which the application relates comes into the person’s possession, custody
or control;

20.8.b

produce to a constable within a specified period for seizure and retention any
material to which the application relates which comes into the person’s
possession, custody or control;

20.8.c

give a constable access to any material of the kind mentioned in paragraph
(b)  within a specified period;

20.8.d

state to the best of the person’s knowledge and belief the location of the
material to which the application relates if it is not in, and will not come
into, the person’s possession, custody or control within the period of 28 days
beginning with the date of the order.

20.9

The specified period is to be the period of 7 days beginning with the date of
the notification required by sub-paragraph  (8) (a) , unless it appears to the
sheriff that a different period would be appropriate in the particular
circumstances of the application.

20.10

Where the sheriff makes an order under  sub-paragraph (8) (c)  in relation to
material on any premises, they may, on the application of the procurator
fiscal, order any person who appears to them to be entitled to grant entry to
the premises to allow any constable to enter the premises to obtain access to
the material.

###### Production orders: supplementary

21

An application for an order under paragraph  19  or  20  may be made without
notice to a sheriff in chambers.

22

22.1

An order under paragraph  19  or  20  has effect despite any obligation as to
secrecy or other restriction on the disclosure of information imposed by an
enactment or otherwise.

22.2

Where the material consists of information stored in electronic form—

22.2.a

an order under paragraph  19 (7) (a)  or  20 (8) (b)  has effect as an order
to produce the material in a form in which it can be taken away and in which
it is visible and legible, or from which it can readily be produced in a
visible and legible form, and

22.2.b

an order under paragraph  19 (7) (b)  or  20 (8) (c)  has effect as an order
to give a constable access to the material in a form in which it is visible
and legible.

23

23.1

An order under paragraph  19  or  20  may be made in relation to material in
the possession, custody or control of a government department.

23.2

Where an order is made by virtue of sub-paragraph  (1) —

23.2.a

it is to be served as if the proceedings were civil proceedings against the
department, and

23.2.b

it may require any officer of the department, whether named in the order or
not, who may for the time being have in their possession, custody or control
the material concerned, to comply with the order.

23.3

In  this paragraph  “ government department ” means—

23.3.a

a public department within the meaning of the Crown Suits (Scotland) Act 1857,
and

23.3.b

any part of the Scottish Administration.

24

24.1

Without prejudice to section 305 of the Criminal Procedure (Scotland) Act
1995, provision may be made by the High Court of Justiciary by Act of
Adjournal as to—

24.1.a

the recall and variation of orders under paragraph  19  or  20 ; and

24.1.b

proceedings relating to such orders.

24.2

The following provisions have effect pending the coming into force of an Act
of Adjournal under sub-paragraph  (1) —

24.2.a

an order under paragraph  19  or  20  may be recalled or varied by a sheriff
on a written application made to the sheriff by any person subject to the
order;

24.2.b

unless the sheriff otherwise directs on grounds of urgency, the applicant
must, not less than 48 hours before making the application, send a copy of it
and a notice in writing of the time and place where the application is to be
made to the procurator fiscal on whose application the order was made.

###### Search, seizure and retention

25

25.1

The procurator fiscal may apply to a sheriff for the issue of a warrant under
this paragraph .

25.2

The sheriff may grant the application if satisfied that an order made under
paragraph 19  or  20  in relation to material on the relevant premises has not
been complied with.

25.3

The sheriff may also grant the application if satisfied that—

25.3.a

conditions 1 to 5 are met, and

25.3.b

in the case of an application for an all premises warrant, condition 6 is met.

25.4

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

25.5

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that there is
on the relevant premises material which is likely to be evidence that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

25.6

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that the
material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

25.7

Condition 4 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be obtained having regard to—

25.7.a

the benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is obtained,
and

25.7.b

the circumstances under which the person concerned has any of the material in
their possession, custody or control.

25.8

Condition 5 is that any of the following apply—

25.8.a

it is not practicable to communicate with any person entitled to produce the
material;

25.8.b

it is not practicable to communicate with any person entitled to grant access
to the material;

25.8.c

the investigation may be seriously prejudiced unless a constable can secure
immediate access to the material.

25.9

Condition 6 is that it is not reasonably practicable to specify in the
application all the premises which the person specified in the application
occupies or controls and which might need to be searched.

25.10

A warrant under  this paragraph  is a warrant authorising any constable—

25.10.a

to enter the relevant premises,

25.10.b

to search the relevant premises and any person found there, and

25.10.c

to seize and retain any material found on a search under paragraph  (b)  which
is likely to be evidence that a relevant act has been, or is about to be,
committed.

25.11

In  this paragraph  the “relevant premises” are—

25.11.a

one or more sets of premises specified in the application (in which case the
application is for a “specific premises warrant”), or

25.11.b

any premises occupied or controlled by a person specified in the application,
including such sets of premises as are so specified (in which case the
application is for an “all premises warrant”).

###### Explanations

26

26.1

The procurator fiscal may apply to a sheriff for an order under  this
paragraph  requiring any person specified in the order to provide an
explanation of material—

26.1.a

seized under a warrant under paragraph  25 , or

26.1.b

produced or made available to a constable under paragraph  19  or  20 .

26.2

An application for an order under this paragraph may be made without notice to
a sheriff in chambers.

26.3

Without prejudice to paragraph  30 , an order under  this paragraph  may
require a lawyer to provide the name and address of their client.

26.4

A statement by a person in response to a requirement imposed by an order under
this paragraph  may only be used in evidence against the person—

26.4.a

on a prosecution for an offence under section 44(2) of the Criminal Law
(Consolidation) (Scotland) Act 1995, or

26.4.b

on a prosecution for some other offence where in giving evidence they make a
statement inconsistent with it.

26.5

Paragraphs 23  and  24  apply to orders under  this paragraph  as they apply
to orders made under  paragraph 19  or  20 .

###### Urgent cases

27

27.1

A police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may by a written order
signed by them give to any constable the authority which may be given by a
warrant under  paragraph 25  (subject to  sub-paragraph (2) ).

27.2

An order under  this paragraph  does not authorise a constable to retain
confidential journalistic material.

27.3

An officer may not make an order under  this paragraph  unless the officer—

27.3.a

is satisfied as mentioned in  paragraph 25 (2)  or  (3) , and

27.3.b

has reasonable grounds for believing that the case is one of great emergency
and that immediate action is necessary.

27.4

Where an order is made under  this paragraph  particulars of the case must be
notified as soon as is reasonably practicable to the Secretary of State.

27.5

A person who wilfully obstructs a search under  this paragraph  commits an
offence.

27.6

A person who commits an offence under  sub-paragraph (5)  is liable on summary
conviction to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 3 months or a fine not
exceeding level 4 on the standard scale (or both).

27.7

“ Confidential journalistic material ” has the same meaning as in the
Investigatory Powers Act 2016 (see section 264(6) and (7) of that Act).

28

28.1

This paragraph  applies where confidential journalistic material is seized by
virtue of an order under  paragraph 27 .

28.2

The procurator fiscal may apply to a sheriff for the issue of a warrant under
this paragraph .

28.3

An application under  sub-paragraph (2)  must be made as soon as reasonably
practicable.

28.4

The sheriff may grant an application under  sub-paragraph (2)  if satisfied
that conditions 1 to 3 are met.

28.5

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that a
relevant act has been, or is about to be, committed.

28.6

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that the
material is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or with other
material, to an investigation into whether a relevant act has been, or is
about to be, committed.

28.7

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest that the material should be retained having regard to the
benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the material is retained.

28.8

A warrant under  this paragraph  is a warrant authorising the retention of
confidential journalistic material.

28.9

A warrant under  this paragraph  may impose conditions on the retention and
use of the material.

28.10

If the sheriff does not grant an application for the issue of a warrant under
this paragraph  in relation to any of the material to which the application
relates, the sheriff may direct that the material is—

28.10.a

returned to the person from whom it was seized, or

28.10.b

destroyed.

28.11

“ Confidential journalistic material ” has the same meaning as in  paragraph
27 .

29

29.1

If a police officer of at least the rank of superintendent has reasonable
grounds for believing that the case is one of great emergency the officer may
by a written notice signed by them require any person specified in the notice
to provide an explanation of any material seized in pursuance of an order
under paragraph  27 .

29.2

Paragraph  26 (3)  and  (4)  apply to a notice under  this paragraph  as they
apply to an order under that paragraph.

29.3

A person who fails to comply with a notice under  this paragraph  commits an
offence.

29.4

It is a defence for a person charged with an offence under  sub-paragraph (3)
to show that they had a reasonable excuse for their failure.

29.5

A person is taken to have shown that they had a reasonable excuse for their
failure if—

29.5.a

sufficient evidence of that fact is adduced to raise an issue with respect to
it, and

29.5.b

the contrary is not proved beyond reasonable doubt.

29.6

A person guilty of an offence under  sub-paragraph (3)  is liable on summary
conviction to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 6 months or a fine not
exceeding level 5 on the standard scale (or both).

###### Supplementary

30

30.1

This Part  of  this Schedule  is without prejudice to any rule of law under
which—

30.1.a

communications between a professional legal adviser and their client, or

30.1.b

communications made in connection with or in contemplation of legal
proceedings and for the purposes of those proceedings,

are in legal proceedings protected from disclosure on the ground of
confidentiality.

30.2

For the purpose of exercising any powers conferred on them under  this Part
of  this Schedule  a constable may, if necessary, open lockfast places on
premises which they are entitled to enter in pursuance of an order under
paragraph 19  or  20 , a warrant under  paragraph 25  or an order under
paragraph  27 .

30.3

A search of a person under  this Part  of  this Schedule  may only be carried
out by a person of the same sex.

                 Disclosure orders

Section 24

##### England and Wales and Northern Ireland

###### Introductory

1

1.1

This Part of this Schedule applies in England and Wales and Northern Ireland.

1.2

“ Relevant investigation ” means an investigation into the identification of
relevant property or its movement or use.

1.3

“ Relevant property ” means—

1.3.a

money or other property which is likely to be used for the purposes of foreign
power threat activity, or

1.3.b

proceeds of involvement in foreign power threat activity.

1.4

The reference to proceeds of involvement in foreign power threat activity
includes a reference to any money, other property or benefit in money’s worth,
which wholly or partly, and directly or indirectly, represents the proceeds of
the involvement (including payments or rewards in connection with the
involvement).

1.5

“ Appropriate officer ” means—

1.5.a

a constable, or

1.5.b

a National Crime Agency officer.

###### Disclosure orders

2

2.1

An appropriate officer may apply to a judge for a disclosure order.

2.2

The application must state that a person or property specified in the
application is subject to a relevant investigation and the order is sought for
the purposes of the investigation.

2.3

The judge may grant the application if satisfied that conditions 1 to 3 are
met.

2.4

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that the
property specified in the application is relevant property.

2.5

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that
information which may be provided in compliance with a requirement imposed
under the order is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or
with other information, to the investigation.

2.6

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest for the information to be provided, having regard to the
benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the information is obtained.

2.7

A disclosure order is an order authorising an appropriate officer to give to
any person the officer considers has relevant information notice in writing
requiring the person to do any or all of the following with respect to any
matter relevant to the investigation—

2.7.a

answer questions, either at a time specified in the notice or at once, at a
place so specified;

2.7.b

provide information specified in the notice, by a time and in a manner so
specified;

2.7.c

produce documents, or documents of a description, specified in the notice,
either at or by a time so specified or at once, and in a manner so specified.

2.8

“ Relevant information ” means information (whether or not contained in a
document) which the appropriate officer considers to be relevant to the
investigation.

2.9

A person is not bound to comply with a requirement imposed by a notice given
under a disclosure order unless evidence of authority to give the notice is
produced.

2.10

An appropriate officer may not make an application under this paragraph unless
the officer is a senior officer or is authorised to do so by a senior officer.

###### Supplementary provision

3

3.1

A disclosure order does not confer the right to require a person—

3.1.a

to answer any question,

3.1.b

to provide any information, or

3.1.c

to produce any document or other material,

which the person would be entitled to refuse to answer, provide or produce on
grounds of legal professional privilege in proceedings in the High Court.

3.2

But a lawyer may be required to provide the name and address of a client.

3.3

A disclosure order does not confer the right to require a person to produce
excluded material.

3.4

A disclosure order has effect despite any restriction on the disclosure of
information imposed by an enactment or otherwise.

3.5

An appropriate officer may take copies of any documents produced in compliance
with a requirement to produce them imposed under a disclosure order.

3.6

The documents may be retained for so long as it is necessary to retain them
(as opposed to a copy of them) in connection with the investigation for the
purposes of which the order was made.

3.7

But if an appropriate officer has reasonable grounds for believing that—

3.7.a

the documents may need to be produced for the purposes of any legal
proceedings, and

3.7.b

they might otherwise be unavailable for those purposes,

they may be retained until the proceedings are concluded.

3.8

An appropriate officer may retain documents under sub-paragraph  (7)  only if
the officer is a senior officer or is authorised to do so by a senior officer.

###### Applications

4

An application for a disclosure order may be made without notice to a judge in
chambers.

###### Discharge or variation

5

5.1

An application to discharge or vary a disclosure order may be made to the
Crown Court by—

5.1.a

the person who applied for the order;

5.1.b

any person affected by the order.

5.2

If the application for the disclosure order was made by a constable, an
application to discharge or vary the order may be made by a different
constable.

5.3

If the application for the disclosure order was made by a National Crime
Agency officer, an application to discharge or vary the order may be made by a
different National Crime Agency officer.

5.4

An appropriate officer may not make an application to discharge or vary a
disclosure order unless the officer is a senior officer or is authorised to do
so by a senior officer.

5.5

The Crown Court may—

5.5.a

discharge the order;

5.5.b

vary the order.

###### Rules of court

6

Rules of court may make provision as to the practice and procedure to be
followed in connection with proceedings relating to disclosure orders.

###### Offences

7

7.1

A person commits an offence if without reasonable excuse the person fails to
comply with a requirement imposed under a disclosure order.

7.2

A person guilty of an offence under sub-paragraph  (1)  is liable—

7.2.a

on summary conviction in England and Wales, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding the maximum term for summary offences or a fine (or both);

7.2.b

on summary conviction in Northern Ireland, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding 6 months or a fine not exceeding level 5 on the standard scale (or
both).

7.3

A person commits an offence if, in purported compliance with a requirement
imposed under a disclosure order, the person—

7.3.a

makes a statement which the person knows to be false or misleading in a
material particular, or

7.3.b

recklessly makes a statement which is false or misleading in a material
particular.

7.4

A person guilty of an offence under sub-paragraph  (3)  is liable—

7.4.a

on conviction on indictment, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 2 years
or a fine (or both);

7.4.b

on summary conviction in England and Wales, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding the general limit in a magistrates’ court or a fine (or both);

7.4.c

on summary conviction in Northern Ireland, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding 6 months or a fine not exceeding the statutory maximum (or both).

###### Statements

8

8.1

A statement made by a person in response to a requirement imposed under a
disclosure order may not be used in evidence against that person in criminal
proceedings.

8.2

Sub-paragraph  (1)  does not apply on a prosecution for—

8.2.a

an offence under paragraph  7 (3) ,

8.2.b

an offence under section 5 of the Perjury Act 1911 or Article 10 of the
Perjury (Northern Ireland) Order 1979 ( S.I. 1979/1714 (N.I. 19) ) (false
statements), or

8.2.c

some other offence where, in giving evidence, the person makes a statement
inconsistent with the statement mentioned in sub-paragraph  (1) .

8.3

A statement may not be used against a person by virtue of sub-paragraph  (2)
(c)  unless—

8.3.a

evidence relating to it is adduced, or

8.3.b

a question relating to it is asked,

by or on behalf of the person in the proceedings arising out of the
prosecution.

###### Interpretation

9

9.1

This paragraph applies for the interpretation of this Part of this Schedule.

9.2

“ Disclosure order ” has the meaning given by paragraph  2 .

9.3

“ Judge ” means—

9.3.a

in relation to England and Wales, a judge entitled to exercise the
jurisdiction of the Crown Court;

9.3.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, a judge of the Crown Court.

9.4

“ Senior officer ” means—

9.4.a

a constable of at least the rank of superintendent;

9.4.b

the Director General of the National Crime Agency or any other National Crime
Agency officer authorised by the Director General (whether generally or
specifically) for this purpose.

9.5

“ Document ” means anything in which information of any description is
recorded.

9.6

“Excluded material”—

9.6.a

in relation to England and Wales, has the same meaning as in the Police and
Criminal Evidence Act 1984;

9.6.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, has the same meaning as in the Police and
Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989 ( S.I. 1989/1341 (N.I. 12) ).

9.7

The terms defined in paragraph  1  have the meanings given in that paragraph.

##### Scotland

###### Introductory

10

10.1

This Part of this Schedule applies in Scotland.

10.2

In this Part of this Schedule “ relevant investigation ” and “ relevant
property ” have the same meaning as in Part  1  of this Schedule.

###### Disclosure orders

11

11.1

The Lord Advocate may apply to the High Court of Justiciary for a disclosure
order.

11.2

The application must state that a person or property specified in the
application is subject to a relevant investigation and the order is sought for
the purposes of the investigation.

11.3

The court may grant the application if satisfied that conditions 1 to 3 are
met.

11.4

Condition 1 is that there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that the
property specified in the application is relevant property.

11.5

Condition 2 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that
information which may be provided in compliance with a requirement imposed
under the order is likely to be of substantial value, whether by itself or
with other information, to the investigation.

11.6

Condition 3 is that there are reasonable grounds for believing that it is in
the public interest for the information to be provided, having regard to the
benefit likely to accrue to the investigation if the information is obtained.

11.7

A disclosure order is an order authorising the Lord Advocate to give to any
person the Lord Advocate considers has relevant information notice in writing
requiring the person to do any or all of the following with respect to any
matter relevant to the investigation—

11.7.a

answer questions, either at a time specified in the notice or at once, at a
place so specified;

11.7.b

provide information specified in the notice, by a time and in a manner so
specified;

11.7.c

produce documents, or documents of a description, specified in the notice,
either at or by a time so specified or at once, and in a manner so specified.

11.8

“ Relevant information ” means information (whether or not contained in a
document) which the Lord Advocate considers to be relevant to the
investigation.

11.9

A person is not bound to comply with a requirement imposed by a notice given
under a disclosure order unless evidence of authority to give the notice is
produced.

###### Supplementary provision

12

12.1

A disclosure order does not confer the right to require a person—

12.1.a

to answer any question,

12.1.b

to provide any information, or

12.1.c

to produce any document,

which the person would be entitled to refuse to answer, provide or produce in
legal proceedings on grounds of confidentiality of communications.

12.2

A disclosure order has effect despite any obligation as to secrecy or other
restriction on the disclosure of information imposed by an enactment or
otherwise.

12.3

The Lord Advocate may take copies of any documents produced in compliance with
a requirement to produce them imposed under a disclosure order.

12.4

The documents may be retained for so long as it is necessary to retain them
(as opposed to a copy of them) in connection with the investigation for the
purposes of which the order was made.

12.5

But if the Lord Advocate has reasonable grounds for believing that—

12.5.a

the documents may need to be produced for the purposes of any legal
proceedings, and

12.5.b

they might otherwise be unavailable for those purposes,

they may be retained until the proceedings are concluded.

###### Applications

13

An application for a disclosure order may be made without notice to a judge of
the High Court of Justiciary.

###### Discharge or variation

14

14.1

An application to discharge or vary a disclosure order may be made to the High
Court of Justiciary by—

14.1.a

the Lord Advocate;

14.1.b

any person affected by the order.

14.2

The High Court of Justiciary may—

14.2.a

discharge the order;

14.2.b

vary the order.

###### Rules of court

15

15.1

Provision may be made in rules of court as to the discharge and variation of
disclosure orders.

15.2

Rules of court are, without prejudice to section 305 of the Criminal Procedure
(Scotland) Act 1995, to be made by Act of Adjournal.

###### Offences

16

16.1

A person commits an offence if without reasonable excuse the person fails to
comply with a requirement imposed under a disclosure order.

16.2

A person guilty of an offence under sub-paragraph  (1)  is liable on summary
conviction to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 6 months or a fine not
exceeding level 5 on the standard scale (or both).

16.3

A person commits an offence if, in purported compliance with a requirement
imposed under a disclosure order, the person—

16.3.a

makes a statement which the person knows to be false or misleading in a
material particular, or

16.3.b

recklessly makes a statement which is false or misleading in a material
particular.

16.4

A person guilty of an offence under sub-paragraph  (3)  is liable—

16.4.a

on conviction on indictment, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding two
years or a fine (or both);

16.4.b

on summary conviction, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 12 months or a
fine not exceeding the statutory maximum (or both).

###### Statements

17

17.1

A statement made by a person in response to a requirement imposed under a
disclosure order may not be used in evidence against that person in criminal
proceedings.

17.2

Sub-paragraph (1) does not apply on a prosecution for—

17.2.a

an offence under paragraph  16 (3) ,

17.2.b

perjury, or

17.2.c

some other offence where, in giving evidence, the person makes a statement
inconsistent with the statement mentioned in sub-paragraph  (1) .

17.3

A statement may not be used against a person by virtue of sub-paragraph  (2)
(c)  unless—

17.3.a

evidence relating to it is adduced, or

17.3.b

a question relating to it is asked,

by or on behalf of the person in the proceedings arising out of the
prosecution.

###### Interpretation

18

18.1

This paragraph applies for the interpretation of this Part of this Schedule.

18.2

“ Disclosure order ” has the meaning given by paragraph  11 .

18.3

“ Document ” means anything in which information of any description is
recorded.

                 Customer information orders

Section 25

##### Customer information orders

1

1.1

An appropriate officer may apply to a judge for a customer information order.

1.2

The judge may grant the application if satisfied that—

1.2.a

the order is sought for the purposes of an investigation into foreign power
threat activity, and

1.2.b

the order will enhance the effectiveness of the investigation.

1.3

“ Appropriate officer ” means—

1.3.a

in relation to England and Wales or Northern Ireland, a constable or a
National Crime Agency officer;

1.3.b

in relation to Scotland, the procurator fiscal.

1.4

The application must state that—

1.4.a

a person specified in the application is subject to an investigation within
sub-paragraph  (2) (a)  and the order is sought for the purposes of the
investigation;

1.4.b

the order is sought against the financial institution or financial
institutions specified in the application.

1.5

The application may specify—

1.5.a

all financial institutions,

1.5.b

a particular description, or particular descriptions, of financial
institutions, or

1.5.c

a particular financial institution or particular financial institutions.

1.6

A customer information order is an order authorising an appropriate officer to
give to a financial institution covered by the application notice in writing
requiring it to provide any customer information it has relating to the person
specified in the application.

1.7

The financial institution must provide the information at or by the time, and
in a manner, specified in the notice.

1.8

A financial institution is not bound to comply with a requirement imposed by a
notice given under a customer information order unless evidence of authority
to give the notice is produced.

1.9

An appropriate officer may not make an application under this paragraph unless
the officer is a senior officer or is authorised to do so by a senior officer.

1.10

Sub-paragraph  (9)  does not apply in relation to Scotland.

##### Supplementary provision

2

A customer information order has effect despite any obligation as to secrecy
or other restriction on the disclosure of information imposed by an enactment
or otherwise.

##### Applications

3

An application for a customer information order may be made without notice to
a judge in chambers.

##### Discharge or variation

4

4.1

An application to discharge or vary a customer information order may be made
to the court by—

4.1.a

the person who applied for the order;

4.1.b

any person affected by the order.

4.2

If the application for the customer information order was made by a constable,
an application to discharge or vary the order may be made by a different
constable.

4.3

If the application for the customer information order was made by a National
Crime Agency officer, an application to discharge or vary the order may be
made by a different National Crime Agency officer.

4.4

An appropriate officer may not make an application under this paragraph unless
the officer is a senior officer or is authorised to do so by a senior officer.

4.5

Sub-paragraph  (4)  does not apply in relation to Scotland.

4.6

The court may—

4.6.a

discharge the order;

4.6.b

vary the order.

##### Rules of court

5

5.1

Rules of court may make provision as to the practice and procedure to be
followed in connection with proceedings relating to customer information
orders.

5.2

In Scotland rules of court are, without prejudice to section 305 of the
Criminal Procedure (Scotland) Act 1995, to be made by Act of Adjournal.

##### Offences

6

6.1

A person commits an offence if without reasonable excuse the person fails to
comply with a requirement imposed under a customer information order.

6.2

A person guilty of an offence under sub-paragraph  (1)  is liable—

6.2.a

on summary conviction in England and Wales, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding the maximum term for summary offences or a fine (or both);

6.2.b

on summary conviction in Northern Ireland, to imprisonment for a term not
exceeding 6 months or a fine not exceeding level 5 on the standard scale (or
both);

6.2.c

on summary conviction in Scotland, to imprisonment for a term not exceeding 6
months or a fine not exceeding level 5 on the standard scale (or both).

##### Statements

7

7.1

A statement made by a person in response to a requirement imposed under a
customer information order may not be used in evidence against them in
criminal proceedings.

7.2

Sub-paragraph  (1)  does not apply on a prosecution for an offence where, in
giving evidence, the person makes a statement inconsistent with the statement
mentioned in sub-paragraph  (1) .

7.3

A statement may not be used against a person by virtue of sub-paragraph  (2)
unless—

7.3.a

evidence relating to it is adduced, or

7.3.b

a question relating to it is asked,

by or on behalf of the person in the proceedings arising out of the
prosecution.

##### Interpretation

8

8.1

This paragraph applies for the interpretation of this Schedule.

8.2

“ Appropriate officer ” has the meaning given by paragraph  1 (3) .

8.3

“ The court ” means—

8.3.a

in relation to England and Wales or Northern Ireland, the Crown Court;

8.3.b

in relation to Scotland, the sheriff.

8.4

“Customer information”—

8.4.a

in relation to England and Wales or Northern Ireland, has the meaning given by
section 364 of the Proceeds of Crime Act 2002;

8.4.b

in relation to Scotland, has the meaning given by section 398 of that Act.

8.5

“ Financial institution ” has the same meaning as in Schedule 6 to the
Terrorism Act 2000 (see paragraph 6 of that Schedule).

8.6

“ Judge ” means—

8.6.a

in relation to England and Wales, a judge entitled to exercise the
jurisdiction of the Crown Court;

8.6.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, a judge of the Crown Court;

8.6.c

in relation to Scotland, the sheriff.

8.7

“ Senior officer ” means—

8.7.a

a constable of at least the rank of superintendent;

8.7.b

the Director General of the National Crime Agency or any other National Crime
Agency officer authorised by the Director General (whether generally or
specifically) for this purpose.

                 Account monitoring orders

Section 26

##### Account monitoring orders

1

1.1

An appropriate officer may apply to a judge for an account monitoring order.

1.2

The judge may grant the application if satisfied that—

1.2.a

the order is sought for the purposes of an investigation into foreign power
threat activity, and

1.2.b

the order will enhance the effectiveness of the investigation.

1.3

“ Appropriate officer ” means—

1.3.a

in relation to England and Wales or Northern Ireland, a constable or a
National Crime Agency officer;

1.3.b

in relation to Scotland, the procurator fiscal.

1.4

The application must state that the order is sought against the financial
institution specified in the application in relation to information which—

1.4.a

relates to an account or accounts held at the institution by the person
specified in the application (whether solely or jointly with another), and

1.4.b

is of the description so specified.

1.5

The application may specify information relating to—

1.5.a

all accounts held by the person specified in the application at the financial
institution so specified,

1.5.b

a particular description, or particular descriptions, of accounts so held, or

1.5.c

a particular account, or particular accounts, so held.

1.6

An account monitoring order is an order that the financial institution
specified in the application must—

1.6.a

for the period specified in the order,

1.6.b

in the manner so specified,

1.6.c

at or by the time or times so specified, and

1.6.d

at the place or places so specified,

provide information of the description specified in the application to an
appropriate officer.

1.7

The period stated in an account monitoring order must not exceed the period of
90 days beginning with the day on which the order is made.

##### Applications

2

An application for an account monitoring order may be made without notice to a
judge in chambers.

##### Discharge or variation

3

3.1

An application to discharge or vary an account monitoring order may be made to
the court by—

3.1.a

the person who applied for the order;

3.1.b

any person affected by the order.

3.2

If the application for the account monitoring order was made by a constable,
an application to discharge or vary the order may be made by a different
constable.

3.3

If the application for the account monitoring order was made by a National
Crime Agency officer, an application to discharge or vary the order may be
made by a different National Crime Agency officer.

3.4

The court may—

3.4.a

discharge the order;

3.4.b

vary the order.

##### Rules of court

4

4.1

Rules of court may make provision as to the practice and procedure to be
followed in connection with proceedings relating to account monitoring orders.

4.2

In Scotland rules of court are, without prejudice to section 305 of the
Criminal Procedure (Scotland) Act 1995, to be made by Act of Adjournal.

##### Effect of orders

5

5.1

In England and Wales and Northern Ireland, an account monitoring order has
effect as if it were an order of the court.

5.2

An account monitoring order has effect in spite of any obligation as to
secrecy or other restriction on the disclosure of information imposed by an
enactment or otherwise.

##### Statements

6

6.1

A statement made by a person in response to an account monitoring order may
not be used in evidence against them in criminal proceedings.

6.2

But sub-paragraph (1) does not apply—

6.2.a

in the case of proceedings for contempt of court;

6.2.b

on a prosecution for an offence where, in giving evidence, the person makes a
statement inconsistent with the statement mentioned in sub-paragraph  (1) .

6.3

A statement may not be used against a person by virtue of sub-paragraph  (2)
(b)  unless—

6.3.a

evidence relating to it is adduced, or

6.3.b

a question relating to it is asked,

by or on behalf of the person in the proceedings arising out of the
prosecution.

##### Interpretation

7

7.1

This paragraph applies for the interpretation of this Schedule.

7.2

“ Appropriate officer ” has the meaning given by paragraph  1 (3) .

7.3

“ The court ” means—

7.3.a

in relation to England and Wales or Northern Ireland, the Crown Court;

7.3.b

in relation to Scotland, the sheriff.

7.4

“ Financial institution ” has the same meaning as in Schedule 6 to the
Terrorism Act 2000 (see paragraph 6 of that Schedule).

7.5

“ Judge ” means—

7.5.a

in relation to England and Wales, a judge entitled to exercise the
jurisdiction of the Crown Court;

7.5.b

in relation to Northern Ireland, a judge of the Crown Court;

7.5.c

in relation to Scotland, the sheriff.

Detention under  section 27

Section 27

##### Treatment of persons detained under 

###### Place of detention

1

1.1

The Secretary of State may designate places at which persons may be detained
under  section 27 .

1.2

The power in sub-paragraph  (1)  may be exercised only in relation to land or
a building in the United Kingdom which is owned or controlled by a police
force.

1.3

In  this Schedule  a reference to a police station includes a reference to any
place which the Secretary of State has designated under  sub-paragraph (1)  as
a place where a person may be detained under  section 27 .

1.4

A constable who arrests a person under  section 27  must take the person as
soon as is reasonably practicable to the police station which the constable
considers the most appropriate.

1.5

Where a person is arrested under  section 27  in one part of the United
Kingdom and some or all of the person’s detention under that section takes
place in another part, the provisions of  this Schedule  which apply to
detention in a particular part of the United Kingdom apply in relation to the
person while detained in that part.

1.6

In this paragraph—

“ building ” includes any part of a building; “ police force ” has the same
meaning as in paragraph  28 .

###### Identification

2

2.1

An authorised person may take any steps which are reasonably necessary for—

2.1.a

photographing the detained person,

2.1.b

measuring the detained person, or

2.1.c

identifying the detained person.

2.2

In  sub-paragraph (1)  “ authorised person ” means any of the following—

2.2.a

a constable;

2.2.b

a prison officer;

2.2.c

a person authorised by the Secretary of State.

2.3

This paragraph  does not confer the power to take—

2.3.a

fingerprints, non-intimate samples or intimate samples (see instead
paragraphs 10  to  14  below), or

2.3.b

relevant physical data or samples as mentioned in section 18 of the Criminal
Procedure (Scotland) Act 1995, as applied by  paragraph 18  below.

###### Video recording of interviews

3

3.1

This paragraph  applies to any interview by a constable of a detained person
that takes place in a police station.

3.2

The Secretary of State must—

3.2.a

issue a code of practice about the video recording of interviews to which
this paragraph  applies, and

3.2.b

make regulations requiring the video recording of interviews to which  this
paragraph  applies in accordance with any relevant code of practice under
paragraph (a) .

3.3

Regulations under  sub-paragraph (2)  must state that the video recording is
to be with sound.

3.4

A code of practice under  this paragraph —

3.4.a

may make provision in relation to a particular part of the United Kingdom, and

3.4.b

may make different provision for different parts of the United Kingdom.

3.5

The failure by a constable to observe a provision of a code does not of itself
make the constable liable to criminal or civil proceedings.

3.6

A code—

3.6.a

is admissible in evidence in criminal and civil proceedings, and

3.6.b

is to be taken into account by a court or tribunal in any case in which it
appears to the court or tribunal to be relevant.

3.7

The Secretary of State may revise a code and issue the revised code.

4

4.1

Before issuing a code of practice under  paragraph 3 , the Secretary of State
must—

4.1.a

publish a draft code,

4.1.b

consider any representations made about the draft, and

4.1.c

if the Secretary of State thinks it appropriate, modify the draft in the light
of any such representations.

4.2

The Secretary of State must lay a draft of the code before Parliament.

4.3

After the code has been laid before Parliament the Secretary of State may
bring it into operation by regulations.

4.4

Sub-paragraphs (1)  to  (3)  apply to the issue of a revised code as they
apply to the first issue of the code.

###### Status

5

A detained person is to be treated as being in legal custody throughout the
period of the person’s detention.

##### Rights of persons detained under 

###### Right to have named person informed of detention

6

6.1

Subject to  paragraph 9 , a person detained under  section 27  at a place in
England, Wales or Northern Ireland is entitled, if the person so requests, to
have one named person informed as soon as is reasonably practicable that the
person is being detained there.

6.2

The person named must be—

6.2.a

a friend of the detained person,

6.2.b

a relative of the detained person, or

6.2.c

a person who is known to the detained person or who is likely to take an
interest in the detained person’s welfare.

6.3

A detained person must be informed of the right under  this paragraph  on
first being detained.

6.4

Where a detained person is transferred from one place to another, the person
is entitled to exercise the right under  this paragraph  in respect of the
place to which the person is transferred.

###### Right to consult a solicitor

7

7.1

Subject to  paragraph 9 , a person detained under  section 27  in England,
Wales or Northern Ireland is entitled, if the person so requests, to consult a
solicitor as soon as is reasonably practicable, privately and at any time.

7.2

Where a request is made under  sub-paragraph (1) , a record must be made of
the request and the time at which it was made.

7.3

A detained person must be informed of the right under  this paragraph  on
first being detained.

8

8.1

Where a person exercises the right under  paragraph 7  to consult a solicitor,
a police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may direct that—

8.1.a

the person may not consult the solicitor who attends for the purpose of the
consultation, or who would so attend but for the giving of the direction, but

8.1.b

the person may consult a different solicitor of the person’s choosing.

8.2

A direction under  this paragraph  may be given before or after a person’s
consultation with a solicitor has started (and if given after it has started,
the right to further consult that solicitor ceases on the giving of the
direction).

8.3

An officer may give a direction under  this paragraph  only if the officer has
reasonable grounds for believing that—

8.3.a

unless the direction is given, the person’s consultation with the solicitor
will have any of the consequences specified in  sub-paragraph (4) , or

8.3.b

the person has benefited from their criminal conduct and that, unless the
direction is given, the person’s consultation with the solicitor will hinder
the recovery of the value of the property constituting the benefit.

8.4

Those consequences are—

8.4.a

interference with or harm to evidence of an indictable offence,

8.4.b

interference with or physical injury to any person,

8.4.c

the alerting of persons who are suspected of having committed an indictable
offence but who have not been arrested for it,

8.4.d

the hindering of the recovery of property obtained as a result of an
indictable offence,

8.4.e

interference with the gathering of information about a person’s involvement in
foreign power threat activity,

8.4.f

making it more difficult, by the alerting of a person, to prevent foreign
power threat activity, and

8.4.g

making it more difficult, by the alerting of a person, to secure a person’s
apprehension, prosecution or conviction in connection with the person’s
involvement in foreign power threat activity.

8.5

For the purposes of  sub-paragraph (3) (b) , the question whether a person has
benefited from their criminal conduct is to be decided in accordance with Part
2 or 4 of the Proceeds of Crime Act 2002.

###### Delay in exercise of rights

9

9.1

A police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may authorise a delay—

9.1.a

in informing the person named by a detained person under  paragraph 6 ;

9.1.b

in permitting a detained person to consult a solicitor under  paragraph 7 .

9.2

But the detained person must be permitted to exercise the rights under
paragraphs 6  and  7  before the end of the period mentioned in  section 27
(3) .

9.3

An officer may authorise a delay under  sub-paragraph (1)  only if the officer
has reasonable grounds for believing—

9.3.a

in the case of an authorisation under  sub-paragraph (1) (a) , that informing
the named person of the detained person’s detention will have any of the
consequences specified in  paragraph 8 (4) , or

9.3.b

in the case of an authorisation under  sub-paragraph (1) (b) , that the
exercise of the right under  paragraph 7  at the time when the detained person
desires to exercise it will have any of the consequences specified in
paragraph 8 (4) .

9.4

An officer may also authorise a delay under  sub-paragraph (1)  if the officer
has reasonable grounds for believing that—

9.4.a

the detained person has benefited from their criminal conduct, and

9.4.b

the recovery of the value of the property constituting the benefit will be
hindered by—

9.4.b.i

informing the named person of the detained person’s detention (in the case of
an authorisation under  sub-paragraph (1) (a) ), or

9.4.b.ii

the detained person’s consultation with a solicitor (in the case of an
authorisation under  sub-paragraph (1) (b) ).

9.5

For the purposes of  sub-paragraph (4) , whether the detained person has
benefited from their criminal conduct is to be decided in accordance with Part
2 or 4 of the Proceeds of Crime Act 2002.

9.6

Where an officer authorises a delay under  sub-paragraph (1)  orally, the
officer must confirm it in writing as soon as is reasonably practicable.

9.7

Where an officer authorises a delay under  sub-paragraph (1) —

9.7.a

the detained person must be told the reason for the delay as soon as is
reasonably practicable, and

9.7.b

the reason must be recorded as soon as is reasonably practicable.

9.8

Where the reason for authorising delay no longer applies, there may be no
further delay in permitting the exercise of the right in the absence of a
further authorisation under  sub-paragraph (1) .

###### Taking of intimate and non-intimate samples

10

10.1

This paragraph  applies where a person is detained under  section 27  in
England, Wales or Northern Ireland.

10.2

Fingerprints may be taken from the detained person only if they are taken by a
constable—

10.2.a

with the appropriate consent given in writing, or

10.2.b

without that consent under  sub-paragraph (4) .

10.3

A non-intimate sample may be taken from the detained person only if it is
taken by a constable—

10.3.a

with the appropriate consent given in writing, or

10.3.b

without that consent under  sub-paragraph (4) .

10.4

Fingerprints or a non-intimate sample may be taken from the detained person
without the appropriate consent only if—

10.4.a

the person is detained at a police station and a police officer of at least
the rank of superintendent authorises the fingerprints or sample to be taken
(but see  sub-paragraphs (6)  and  (7) ), or

10.4.b

the person has been convicted of a recordable offence and, where a non-
intimate sample is to be taken, the person was convicted of the offence on or
after 10th April 1995 (or 29th July 1996 where the non-intimate sample is to
be taken in Northern Ireland).

10.5

An intimate sample may be taken from the detained person only if—

10.5.a

the person is detained at a police station,

10.5.b

the appropriate consent is given in writing,

10.5.c

a police officer of at least the rank of superintendent authorises the sample
to be taken (but see  sub-paragraph (6) ), and

10.5.d

subject to  paragraph 13 (2)  and  (3) , the sample is taken by a constable.

10.6

An officer may give an authorisation under  sub-paragraph (4) (a)  or  (5) (c)
only if—

10.6.a

the officer reasonably suspects that the detained person has been involved in
foreign power threat activity, and

10.6.b

the officer reasonably believes that the fingerprints or sample will tend to
confirm or disprove the person’s involvement.

10.7

An officer may also give an authorisation under  sub-paragraph (4) (a)  for
the taking of fingerprints if—

10.7.a

the officer is satisfied that the detained person’s fingerprints will help
determine the person’s identity, and

10.7.b

the person has refused to identify themselves or the officer has reasonable
grounds for suspecting that the person is not who they claim to be.

10.8

In  this paragraph  references to determining a person’s identity include
showing that the detained person is not a particular person.

10.9

If an authorisation under  sub-paragraph (4) (a)  or  (5) (c)  is given
orally, the person giving it must confirm it in writing as soon as is
reasonably practicable.

11

11.1

Before fingerprints or a sample are taken from a detained person under
paragraph 10 , the person must be informed—

11.1.a

that the fingerprints or sample may be used for the purposes of—

11.1.a.i

a relevant search (within the meaning given by  paragraph 19 (6) ),

11.1.a.ii

section 63A(1) of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984 (checking of
fingerprints and samples), or

11.1.a.iii

Article 63A(1) of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order
1989 ( S.I. 1989/1341 (N.I. 12) ) (checking of fingerprints and samples), and

11.1.b

where the fingerprints or sample are to be taken under  paragraph 10 (2) (a) ,
(3) (a)  or  (4) (b) , of the reason for taking the fingerprints or sample.

11.2

Before fingerprints or a sample are taken from a person on an authorisation
given under  paragraph 10 (4) (a)  or  (5) (c) , the person must be informed—

11.2.a

that the authorisation has been given,

11.2.b

of the grounds upon which it has been given, and

11.2.c

where relevant, of the nature of the offence in which the person is suspected
of having been involved.

11.3

After fingerprints or a sample are taken under  paragraph 10 , any of the
following which apply must be recorded as soon as reasonably practicable—

11.3.a

the fact that the person has been informed in accordance with  sub-paragraphs
(1)  and  (2) ,

11.3.b

the reason referred to in  sub-paragraph (1) (b) ,

11.3.c

the authorisation given under  paragraph 10 (4) (a)  or  (5) (c) ,

11.3.d

the grounds on which that authorisation has been given, and

11.3.e

the fact that the appropriate consent has been given.

12

12.1

This paragraph  applies where—

12.1.a

two or more non-intimate samples suitable for the same means of analysis have
been taken from a detained person under  paragraph 10 ,

12.1.b

those samples have proved insufficient, and

12.1.c

the person has been released from detention.

12.2

An intimate sample may be taken from the person if—

12.2.a

the appropriate consent is given in writing,

12.2.b

a police officer of at least the rank of superintendent authorises the sample
to be taken, and

12.2.c

subject to  paragraph 13 (2)  and  (3) , the sample is taken by a constable.

12.3

Paragraphs 10 (6)  and (9) and  11  apply in relation to the taking of an
intimate sample under  this paragraph  as if references to a detained person
are references to a person who was detained under  section 27  when the non-
intimate samples mentioned in  sub-paragraph (1) (a)  were taken.

13

13.1

Where appropriate written consent to the taking of an intimate sample from a
person under  paragraph 10  or  12  is refused without good cause, in any
proceedings against that person for an offence—

13.1.a

the court, in determining whether to commit the person for trial or whether
there is a case to answer, may draw such inferences from the refusal as appear
proper, and

13.1.b

the court or jury, in determining whether that person is guilty of the offence
charged, may draw such inferences from the refusal as appear proper.

13.2

An intimate sample other than a sample of urine or a dental impression may be
taken under  paragraph 10  or  12  only by a registered medical practitioner
acting on the authority of a constable.

13.3

An intimate sample which is a dental impression may be taken under  paragraph
10  or  12  only by a registered dentist acting on the authority of a
constable.

13.4

Where a sample of hair other than pubic hair is to be taken under  paragraph
10 , the sample may be taken either by cutting hairs or by plucking hairs with
their roots so long as no more are plucked than the person taking the sample
reasonably considers to be necessary for a sufficient sample.

14

14.1

In the application of  paragraphs 10  to  13  in relation to a person detained
in England or Wales, the following expressions have the meaning given by
section 65 of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984—

14.1.a

“appropriate consent”,

14.1.b

“fingerprints”,

14.1.c

“insufficient”,

14.1.d

“intimate sample”,

14.1.e

“non-intimate sample”,

14.1.f

“registered dentist”, and

14.1.g

“sufficient”.

14.2

In the application of section 65(2A) of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act
1984 for the purposes of sub-paragraph  (1)  of  this paragraph , the
reference to the destruction of a sample under section 63R of that Act is a
reference to the destruction of a sample under  paragraph 25  of  this
Schedule .

14.3

In the application of  paragraphs 10  to  13  in relation to a person detained
in Northern Ireland, the expressions listed in  sub-paragraph (1)  have the
meaning given by Article 53 of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern
Ireland) Order 1989 ( S.I. 1989/1341 (N.I. 12) ).

14.4

In  paragraph 10  “recordable offence”—

14.4.a

in relation to a person detained in England or Wales, has the meaning given by
section 118(1) of the Police and Criminal Evidence Act 1984, and

14.4.b

in relation to a person detained in Northern Ireland, has the meaning given by
Article 2(2) of the Police and Criminal Evidence (Northern Ireland) Order 1989
( S.I. 1989/1341 (N.I. 12) ).

##### Rights of persons detained under 

15

15.1

A person detained under  section 27  at a place in Scotland is entitled to
have intimation of the person’s detention and of the place where the person is
being detained sent without delay to a solicitor and to another person named
by the detained person.

15.2

The person named must be—

15.2.a

a friend of the detained person,

15.2.b

a relative of the detained person, or

15.2.c

a person who is known to the detained person or who is likely to take an
interest in the detained person’s welfare.

15.3

Where a detained person is transferred from one place to another, the person
is entitled to exercise the right under  sub-paragraph (1)  in respect of the
place to which the person is transferred.

15.4

A police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may authorise a delay
in making intimation where, in the officer’s view, the delay is necessary on
one of the grounds mentioned in  paragraph 16 (3)  or where  paragraph 16 (4)
applies.

15.5

Where a detained person requests that the intimation be made, a record must be
made of the time at which the request was made and complied with.

15.6

A person who is detained as mentioned in  sub-paragraph (1)  is entitled to
consult a solicitor at any time, without delay.

15.7

A police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may authorise a delay
in holding the consultation where, in the officer’s view, the delay is
necessary on one of the grounds mentioned in  paragraph 16 (3)  or where
paragraph 16 (4)  applies.

15.8

The consultation is to be private.

15.9

A detained person must be permitted to exercise the rights under  this
paragraph  before the end of the period mentioned in  section 27 (3) .

15.10

A detained person must be informed of the rights under  sub-paragraphs (1)
and  (6)  on first being detained.

16

16.1

Where a person exercises the right under  paragraph 15 (6)  to consult a
solicitor, a police officer of at least the rank of superintendent may, if it
appears to the officer to be necessary on one of the grounds mentioned in
sub-paragraph (3) , direct that—

16.1.a

the person may not consult the solicitor who attends for the purpose of the
consultation, or who would so attend but for the giving of the direction, but

16.1.b

the person may consult a different solicitor of the person’s choosing.

16.2
